        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.2c20642"><meta property="og:title" content="Realizing High-Performance Cathodes with Cationic and Anionic Redox Reactions in High-Sodium-Content P2-Type Oxides for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/acsami.2c20642.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="P2-type layered transition-metal oxides with anionic redox reactions are promising cathodes for sodium-ion batteries. In this work, a high-sodium-content P2-type Na7/9Li1/9Mg1/9Cu1/9Mn2/3O2 (NLMC) cathode material is prepared by substituting Li/Mg/Cu for Mn sites in Na2/3MnO2. The Li/Mg ions trigger the anionic redox reaction, while the Cu ions enhance the structure stability during electrochemical cycling. As a result, the oxide has a high reversible capacity of 225 mAh g–1 originating from both cationic and anionic redox activities with a capacity retention of 77% after 100 cycles. The migration energy barrier and Na ion diffusion kinetics are studied using density functional theory (DFT) calculations and the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique. Furthermore, X-ray diffraction, DFT, scanning electron microscopy, and transmission electron microscopy are applied to reveal the structural evolution and charge compensation of NLMC, providing a thorough understanding of the structural and morphology evolution of Na-deficient oxides during cycling. The results are inspiring for the design of a high-Na content P2-type layered oxide cathode for sodium-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/acsami.2c20642.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318827cac3154b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318827cac3154b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'd7d25f8f-daa1-4255-99cd-ff6ee0305177'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318827cac3154b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="9318827cac3154b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Realizing High-Performance Cathodes with Cationic and Anionic Redox Reactions in High-Sodium-Content P2-Type Oxides for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c20642", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Realizing High-Performance Cathodes with Cationic and Anionic Redox Reactions in High-Sodium-Content P2-Type Oxides for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c20642", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Realizing High-Performance Cathodes with Cationic and Anionic Redox Reactions in High-Sodium-Content P2-Type Oxides for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c20642", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Realizing High-Performance Cathodes with Cationic and Anionic Redox Reactions in High-Sodium-Content P2-Type Oxides for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c20642", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Realizing High-Performance Cathodes with Cationic and Anionic Redox Reactions in High-Sodium-Content P2-Type Oxides for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c20642", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Realizing High-Performance Cathodes with Cationic and Anionic Redox Reactions in High-Sodium-Content P2-Type Oxides for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c20642", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Realizing High-Performance Cathodes with Cationic and Anionic Redox Reactions in High-Sodium-Content P2-Type Oxides for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c20642", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Realizing High-Performance Cathodes with Cationic and Anionic Redox Reactions in High-Sodium-Content P2-Type Oxides for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c20642", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Realizing High-Performance Cathodes with Cationic and Anionic Redox Reactions in High-Sodium-Content P2-Type Oxides for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.2c20642", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">21</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/medium/am2c20642_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/large/am2c20642_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c20642&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">P2-type layered transition-metal oxides with anionic redox reactions are promising cathodes for sodium-ion batteries. In this work, a high-sodium-content P2-type Na<sub>7/9</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>Mg<sub>1/9</sub>Cu<sub>1/9</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NLMC) cathode material is prepared by substituting Li/Mg/Cu for Mn sites in Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. The Li/Mg ions trigger the anionic redox reaction, while the Cu ions enhance the structure stability during electrochemical cycling. As a result, the oxide has a high reversible capacity of 225 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> originating from both cationic and anionic redox activities with a capacity retention of 77% after 100 cycles. The migration energy barrier and Na ion diffusion kinetics are studied using density functional theory (DFT) calculations and the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique. Furthermore, X-ray diffraction, DFT, scanning electron microscopy, and transmission electron microscopy are applied to reveal the structural evolution and charge compensation of NLMC, providing a thorough understanding of the structural and morphology evolution of Na-deficient oxides during cycling. The results are inspiring for the design of a high-Na content P2-type layered oxide cathode for sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D9324%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DQiong%2BLiu%252C%2BWei%2BZheng%252C%2BGuiyu%2BLiu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D15%26issueNum%3D7%26contentID%3Dacsami.2c20642%26title%3DRealizing%2BHigh-Performance%2BCathodes%2Bwith%2BCationic%2Band%2BAnionic%2BRedox%2BReactions%2Bin%2BHigh-Sodium-Content%2BP2-Type%2BOxides%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D9330%26publicationDate%3DFebruary%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode%20materials">cathode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anionic+Redox+Reaction&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anionic%20redox%20reaction">anionic redox reaction</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na-deficient+Oxides&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na-deficient%20oxides">Na-deficient oxides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Phase+Transition&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_phase%20transition">phase transition</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are some of the most promising candidates for large-scale electrical energy storage systems owing to their low cost, high abundance, and worldwide availability of Na. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 1 to 6">(1−6)</a> For the past few decades, although great attention has been paid to investigating a large number of electrode materials with the desired properties in SIBs, it is still a challenge to realize high-performance SIBs due to the inadequate energy density and life span of the cathode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a> Numerous materials such as oxides, sulfates, cyanides, and phosphates have been explored as cathodes for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 9 to 13">(9−13)</a> Among them, sodium-based layered transition-metal (TM) materials with an O3-type or a P2-type structure have attracted the most interest due to their simple synthesis and high capacities. Although O3-type oxides can deliver a high initial reversible capacity, they suffer from severe capacity decay due to the complex phase transition and high Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy barriers, impeding their application. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14,15)</a> Compared to O3-type materials, P2-type cathodes are more favorable for SIBs due to their large interlayer spacing, high structural stability, and open Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion pathways. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> However, the Na-deficient P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> ranges from 0.5 to 0.7, TM = Fe, Co, Ni, Mn, etc.) layered oxides mostly deliver a low specific capacity and suffer from a P2 to O2 phase transition during electrochemical cycling, which is unsatisfactory for practical applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 17 to 20">(17−20)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">It is a useful strategy to modify the material structure to enhance the phase stability and electrochemical behavior by introducing suitable metal ions. To address the drawbacks of Na-deficient P2 oxides, many studies have been performed via ion substitution (such as Li<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Cr<sup>3+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>, Cu<sup>2+</sup>, Al<sup>3+</sup>, etc.). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 21 to 25">(21−25)</a> For example, Chen’s group found that Mg- and Ti-codoped Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>2/9</sub>Mg<sub>1/9</sub>Mn<sub>5/9</sub>Ti<sub>1/9</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> could regulate the crystal structure and enhance the electrochemical properties by suppressing the phase transition. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Especially, when weak bonds like Li–O, Mg–O, or Zn–O bonds are introduced in the Na-deficient oxides, the anionic redox reaction O<sup>2–</sup>/O<sup><i>n</i>–</sup> could be triggered at a high working potential of around 4.2 V, which opens a new path to improve the specific capacity of these oxides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 27 to 32">(27−32)</a> Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Mg<sub>0.28</sub>Mn<sub>0.72</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> is reported to have a high initial charge/discharge capacity of above 150 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> due to the contribution of the O redox process, which is close to its theoretical capacity based on the Na content of the compound. Similar cases were encountered in Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2/3</sub>Zn<sub>0.28</sub>Mn<sub>0.72</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Therefore, when an anionic redox reaction is introduced in the P2 layered compounds, a further improvement in specific capacity is limited by the Na content in the lattice. Recently, the research groups of Hu and of Wang reported that a high-Na-content layered structure could be successfully achieved in P2-type layered TM oxides: i.e., Na<sub>0.85</sub>Li<sub>0.12</sub>Ni<sub>0.22</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>45/54</sub>Li<sub>4/54</sub>Ni<sub>16/54</sub>Mn<sub>34/54</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> Both compounds exhibit a solid solution reaction which hinders the phase transitions and thus show good cycling performance. However, the structural stability of the compound is at the expense of capacity, where the advantage of high Na content in the material is not fully utilized, as the anionic redox process is limited. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> It is probably an effective strategy to achieve high specific capacity by employing an anionic redox reaction in high-Na-content P2 oxides, but such research is rare.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, the high-Na-content material Na<sub>7/9</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>Mg<sub>1/9</sub>Cu<sub>1/9</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NLMC) is prepared. In the new compound, Li and Mg ions are introduced to trigger the anionic redox reaction while Cu ions are introduced to suppress the P2 to O2 phase transition. The structure and morphology of the material are studied by X-ray diffraction (XRD), scanning electron microscopy (SEM),  high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM), and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), which confirm the structure and the composition of the material. Electrochemical tests show that the compound has a high specific capacity of 225 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> based on cationic and anionic redox processes. The long-term cycling performance shows that it has a capacity retention of 77% after 100 cycles. Our ex situ XRD result shows that it has a suppressed P2 to OP4 phase transition during electrochemical cycling. Furthermore, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) shows that it has a moderate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient, and theoretical studies suggest that the migration barrier from the Na<sub>e</sub>–Na<sub>e</sub> site is smaller than that from the Na<sub>e</sub>–Na<sub>f</sub> path in the lattice. In addition, the structure evolution of the compound during extensive cycling is also revealed.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Structure and Morphology</h3><div class="NLM_p">NLMC was synthesized via a sol–gel method followed by a high-temperature reaction. The Rietveld refined XRD data of the compound are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a> The data show that the oxide powder corresponds mainly to the P2 structure within the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> and <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub> space groups. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b, the addition peaks around 20° might be induced by the ordered arrangement of Li/Mg/Cu/Mn ions in TM layers in the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub> space group. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The SEM and TEM images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c,d show that the compound has a typical hexagon-shaped morphology with a size of between 2 and 3 μm. The HR-TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e) and selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f) reveal that the material has a lattice fringe with a 0.26 nm spacing corresponding to the (100) planes of NLMC. The EDS images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g–k show that the micrometer-sized particle is homogeneously composed of Na, Mg, Cu, Mn, and O.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/medium/am2c20642_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/large/am2c20642_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Rietveld-refined powder XRD patterns of NLMC. (b) Crystallographic structure of the layered P2 structure and the TM layer (yellow, Na; green, Li; blue, Mg; orange, Cu; purple, Mn; red, O). (c) SEM image of NLMC. (d) TEM image. (e) HR-TEM image with lattice fringes. (f) SAED patterns of the sample. (g–k) EDS mapping images.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/large/am2c20642_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c20642&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">XPS was used to evaluate the valence state of the elements in NLMC. Na, Li, Mg, Cu, Mn, and O can be found in the survey spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/suppl_file/am2c20642_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a> shows the high-resolution spectra of Mg, Mn, Cu, and O ions in the compound. The Mg 1s spectrum shows that it has an oxidation state of +2. The Mn 2p spectrum can be deconvoluted into two peaks associated with Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (642.3 eV) and Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (653.8 eV), corresponding to a Mn oxidation state of +4 in the material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> Four characteristic peaks for the Cu 2p spectrum can be found in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c: i.e., Cu 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (933.5 eV), Cu 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (953.2 eV), and their satellite peaks. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41,42)</a> This indicates that Cu is in the Cu<sup>2+</sup> state in the compound. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d illustrates the O 1s spectrum, and it can be fitted to three parts, which can be assigned to the lattice oxygen (529.2 eV) and to surface-adsorbed species (531.3 and 532.8 eV). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/medium/am2c20642_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/large/am2c20642_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. XPS analyses of Na<sub>7/9</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>Mg<sub>1/9</sub>Cu<sub>1/9</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. XPS spectra of (a) Mg 1s, (b) Mn 2p, (c) Cu 2p, and (d) O 1s.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/large/am2c20642_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c20642&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Electrochemical Properties</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical properties of NLMC were investigated using CR2016 coin cells with pure Na as the counter electrode. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a shows the initial two galvanostatic charge/discharge curves for NLMC at a small current of 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 1.5 V–4.4 V. The compound delivered a first charge capacity at around 198 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a two-step process: i.e., a slope below 4.1 V and a long flat plateau above 4.1 V. Because Mn<sup>4+</sup> could not be further oxidized, the slope between 3.1 and 4.1 V is attributed to Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup>, while the plateau around 4.2 V corresponds to the oxygen oxidation process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45,46)</a> When the cell was discharged to 1.5 V, a discharge capacity of 225 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was achieved as more Na<sup>+</sup> ions were inserted into the electrode. In the second cycle, the charge/discharge curves changed a great deal, as Mn ions also worked as redox centers with a charge/discharge capacity of 218/215 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the sample at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 1.5 V and 4.4 V. For the initial charge process, there are two peaks around 3.5 V and 4.2 V, corresponding to the oxidation of Cu and O ions in the lattice, respectively. In the second curve, a new peak around 2.3 V is observed which corresponds to the oxidation of Mn ions in the structure. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c shows the cycling performance of the compound at a current of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. After 100 cycles, the sample still has a capacity of 130 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity retention of 77%. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d shows the rate performance of the sample when it was cycled at different rates ranging from 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup> to 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. At a current of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the sample has a capacity of 110 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which indicates that the compound has a good rate performance.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/medium/am2c20642_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/large/am2c20642_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of NLMC: (a) galvanostatic charge/discharge curve for the first two cycles, (b) CV curves, (c) cycling performance, (d) rate performance, (e) EIS spectra, and (f) GITT results for the first cycle and the calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/large/am2c20642_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c20642&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">To further understand the performance of NLMC, the electrode process kinetics were evaluated by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) spectra and GITT. The Nyquist plot is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e, which indicates that NLMC has a resistance of around 350 Ω. The typical charge/discharge GITT profile for the first cycle and the corresponding Na diffusion coefficients are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f. The <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> values of NLMC range from 10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> to 10<sup>–15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, which is a moderate value for Na-deficient oxides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48 ref49 ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48 ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 47 to 50">(47−50)</a> Also, during charging, an obvious decrease of the Na diffusion coefficient was observed at high potential. In the anionic oxidation process, the preceding anionic activation plateau unavoidably causes some irreversible oxygen loss and permanently modifies the electrochemical profile due to the oxygen network distortion and rearrangement of TM ions. Thus, accompanied by the removal of Na<sup>+</sup> ions from the lattice at high voltage, the bulk structure suffers from distortion or structure reconstruction, which is kinetically unfavorable. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a></div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  DFT Calculations</h3><div class="NLM_p">DFT calculations were applied to explore the phase stability and electronic density with various Na contents to analyze the structural stability and charge transformation during the desodiation/sodiation. A 3 × 3 × 1 supercell containing 68 atoms (14 Na atoms, 2 Li atoms, 2 Mg atoms, 2 Cu atoms, 12 Mn atoms, and 36 O atoms, i.e., Na<sub>14</sub>Li<sub>2</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>O<sub>36</sub>) was optimized and used as the initial structure to investigate the different charge states. First, the formation energies of mixing enthalpy for the intermediate phase Na<sub>14–<i>x</i></sub>Li<sub>2</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>O<sub>36</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 2, 4, 6, 8, 10, 12, and 14) and the end phase Li<sub>2</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>O<sub>36</sub> in a P2 lattice with different Na/vacancy orderings were calculated. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a shows the convex hull curve of NLMC. The energy diagram of Na<sub>14–<i>x</i></sub>Li<sub>2</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>O<sub>36</sub> predicts that there is a two-phase region from <i>x</i> = 14 to <i>x</i> = 10 over the full range. It indicates that the compound would undergo a phase transition when a large amount of Na ions is extracted from the lattice. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b shows the calculated potential profiles based on the ground states varying with Na contents, which is consistent with the experimental profiles shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a. The redox mechanisms in NLMC were identified by the variation of the projected density of states when <i>x</i> = 0, 2, and 4 in Na<sub>(7–<i>x</i>)/9</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>Mg<sub>1/9</sub>Cu<sub>1/9</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c. During the Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation process from the pristine form to Na<sub>5/9</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>Mg<sub>1/9</sub>Cu<sub>1/9</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, the electron densities of the Cu 3d orbitals decreased to approximately zero, which indicates a Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup> redox reaction in these compounds. In Na<sub>5/9</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>Mg<sub>1/9</sub>Cu<sub>1/9</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>1/3</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>Mg<sub>1/9</sub>Cu<sub>1/9</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, the electron densities near <i>E</i><sub>F</sub> are mostly composed of O 2p states. This implies the occurrence of the O<sup>2–</sup>/(O<sub>2</sub>)<sup><i>n</i>−</sup> redox reaction in NLMC, which is consistent with the results above and previous works on Li-ion batteries (LIBs) and SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 52 and 53">(52,53)</a> The diffusion path in NLMC was evaluated by the climbing-image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method. As Na ions in the P2 structure have two independent sites, one is edge-shared O with TM ions in the TM layer (Na<sub>e</sub>) and the other is face-shared O with TM ions in the TM layer (Na<sub>f</sub>), and the diffusion path can be divided into Na<sub>e</sub>–Na<sub>e</sub> and Na<sub>e</sub>–Na<sub>f</sub> paths, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d. It was found that the energy barrier of Na ion migration of the Na<sub>e</sub>–Na<sub>e</sub> path is about 0.747 eV, while that for the Na<sub>e</sub>–Na<sub>f</sub> path is a bit larger (0.913 eV), indicating that Na<sup>+</sup> ions prefer to move from the Na<sub>e</sub> to Na<sub>e</sub> sites.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/medium/am2c20642_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/large/am2c20642_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Phase stability in the Na<sub>14</sub>Li<sub>2</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>O<sub>36</sub>–Li<sub>2</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>O<sub>36</sub> convex hull (red point, ground state; blue point: pseudo ground state). (b) The calculated voltage profile during the desodiation process in Na<sub>14</sub>Li<sub>2</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>O<sub>36</sub>. (c) Electronic structure evolution of the partial density of states in NLMC (d) The diffusion path of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (yellow, Na; green, Li; blue, Mg; orange, Cu; purple, Mn; red, O; red line, Na<sub>e</sub>–Na<sub>e</sub>; blue line, Na<sub>e</sub>–Na<sub>f</sub>) and (e) the diffusion energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in NLMC.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/large/am2c20642_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c20642&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Structural Evolution</h3><div class="NLM_p">To gain further insight into the structural evolution of the compound upon Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion and extraction, ex situ XRD at different cutoff voltage states during the initial cycle was performed. During charging, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, the (002) peak undergoes a small shift to lower angles from 16.15° to 15.80°, whereas the (100) and (102) peaks shift to higher angles and continuously decrease in peak intensity. These variations are related to the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and the oxidation process, which leads to an expansion of the interlayer spacing and a contraction of the TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, respectively. On charging to 4.4 V, large amounts of Na ions are extracted from the lattice, which induces the shift of the TM layer of the compound. As a result, a new peak at around 17.5° appears which indicates that the compound undergoes a phase transition from the P2 to the OP4 lattice at high voltages. During the discharge process, the new peak fades away, and other peaks have an inverse shift to higher or lower angles while the intensity gradually increases. Thus, a reversible P2–OP4–P2 phase transition was observed in the cathode material, which suppresses the damage by gliding of the TM layer and provides a good cycling reversibility. The structure and morphology evolutions of the compound were investigated by ex situ XRD spectra and SEM images after 10, 20, 50, and 100 cycles. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b, with increased cycling, the (002) peak gradually shifts to lower angles, corresponding to an increase of the <i>c</i> lattice parameter. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c–f shows the morphology of the compound after 10, 20, 50, and 100 cycles, respectively. Initially, the compound was coated by a thin film of poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF). After 10 and 20 cycles, the morphology of the compound does not change much. After 50 cycles, cracks of the thin PVDF film could be observed. Moreover, clear cleavages could be found in the compound after 100 cycles, which accounts for the capacity decay during extended cycling. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/suppl_file/am2c20642_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> shows the HR-TEM image of the sample after 100 cycles, and it could be clearly seen that after long-term cycling the compound suffers from some destruction of the layered structure, resulting in the formation of nanovoids in the structure.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/medium/am2c20642_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/large/am2c20642_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Ex situ XRD patterns at various charge/discharge states during the first cycle of NLMC. (b) Ex situ XRD patterns of the cathode material after 0, 10, 20, 50, and 100 cycles. (c–f) Ex situ SEM images of the cathode material after 10, 20, 50, and 100 cycles, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/asset/images/large/am2c20642_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c20642&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">A new high-Na-content P2-type layered oxide with a formulation of NLMC has been successfully prepared. The combined redox process of both the TM and O ions gives the cathode a high initial capacity of 225 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with excellent capacity retention and rate performance. Experimental results demonstrate that the compound has a moderate Na diffusion coefficient, while DFT calculations show that the Na ions prefer to migrate from the Na<sub>e</sub> to the Na<sub>e</sub> site in the lattice, as it has a lower Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier. Further experimental and theoretical analyses indicate that the material undergoes a phase transition from the P2 to the OP4 lattice, favorably suppressing the gliding of the TM layer and thus enhancing the structural stability. The multiconfiguration strategy to enhance the lattice Na concentration and stabilize the anionic redox reversibility is encouraging for the further development of high-performance cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c20642?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c20642</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Experimental section, XPS spectrum, charge/discharge curves for 5, 10, 20, 50, and 100 cycles, and HR-TEM image after 100 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/suppl_file/am2c20642_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c20642/suppl_file/am2c20642_si_001.pdf">am2c20642_si_001.pdf (237.1 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.2c20642" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

The strong and weak points of the main families of compds. for each battery component are analyzed in this progress report.  Taking into account the achievements made in materials for NIBs, the industrial scene is analyzed through the existing prototypes and com. cells and also from an economical viewpoint.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Novel P2-type concentration-gradient Na0.67Ni0.167Co0.167Mn0.67O2 modified by Mn-rich surface as cathode material for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bao, Shuo; Luo, Shao-hua; Wang, Zhi-yuan; Yan, Sheng-xue; Wang, Qing; Li, Jia-yu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">396</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">404-411</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Developing rechargeable sodium ion batteries with high capacity and long cycle life is still a big challenge.  Herein, we present P2-type concn.-gradient material with av. compn. of Na0.67Ni0.167Co0.167Mn0.67O2, which exhibits relatively high discharge capacity and excellent stability.  The material delivers higher discharge capacity and better rate performance than that of the concn.-const. Na0.67Ni0.167Co0.167Mn0.67O2 during cycling.  Also, the material exhibits excellent capacity retention of ∼87% after 100 cycles.  The high discharge capacity is attributed to the Ni-rich core and the improvement in cycling stability is due to a gradual and continuous increase of Mn4+ in the concn.-gradient spherical particles.  The primary particles on the Mn-rich surface have smaller size than that of the const.-concn. samples, leading to a preferential cryst. state, therefore they can facilitate excellent transport properties for Na ions insertion/extn. process.  The P2-type concn.-gradient material provides a new way for the development of advanced sodium ion batteries with high capacity and long cycle life.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202201065"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zong, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Brett, J.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rationally Designed Sodium Chromium Vanadium Phosphate Cathodes with Multi-Electron Reaction for Fast-Charging Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Wei; Wu, Yulun; Xu, Zhenming; Li, Huangxu; Xu, Ming; Li, Jianwei; Dai, Yuhang; Zong, Wei; Chen, Ruwei; He, Liang; Zhang, Zhian; Brett, Dan J. L.; He, Guanjie; Lai, Yanqing; Parkin, Ivan P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">25</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2201065</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium super-ionic conductor (NASICON)-structured phosphates are emerging as rising stars as cathodes for sodium-ion batteries.  However, they usually suffer from a relatively low capacity due to the limited activated redox couples and low intrinsic electronic cond.  Herein, a reduced graphene oxide supported NASICON Na3Cr0.5V1.5(PO4)3 cathode (VC/C-G) is designed, which displays ultrafast (up to 50 C) and ultrastable (1 000 cycles at 20 C) Na+ storage properties.  The VC/C-G can reach a high energy d. of ≈470 W h kg-1 at 0.2 C with a specific capacity of 176 mAh g-1 (equiv. to the theor. value); this corresponds to a three-electron transfer reaction based on fully activated V5+/V4+, V4+/V3+, V3+/V2+ couples.  In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) results disclose a combination of solid-soln. reaction and biphasic reaction mechanisms upon cycling.  D. functional theory calcns. reveal a narrow forbidden-band gap of 1.41 eV and a low Na+ diffusion energy barrier of 0.194 eV.  Furthermore, VC/C-G shows excellent fast-charging performance by only taking ≈11 min to reach 80% state of charge.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A High-Energy NASICON-Type Cathode Material for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jingyang; Wang, Yan; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Shi, Tan; Chen, Shouping; Tian, Yaosen; Kim, Haegyeom; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Over the last decade, Na-ion batteries have been extensively studied as low-cost alternatives to Li-ion batteries for large-scale grid storage applications; however, the development of high-energy pos. electrodes remains a major challenge. Herein, a novel NASICON-type compd., Na4MnCr(PO4)3, is reported as a promising cathode material for Na-ion batteries that deliver a high specific capacity of 130 mAh g-1 during discharge utilizing high-voltage Mn2+/3+ (3.5 V), Mn3+/4+ (4.0 V), and Cr3+/4+ (4.35 V) transition metal redox.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Universal Strategy toward Air-Stable and High-Rate O3 Layered Oxide Cathodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yuan, Xin-Guang; Guo, Yu-Jie; Gan, Lu; Yang, Xin-An; He, Wei-Huan; Zhang, Xu-Sheng; Yin, Ya-Xia; Xin, Sen; Yao, Hu-Rong; Huang, Zhigao; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">As one of the fascinating high capacity cathodes, O3-type layered oxides usually suffer from their intrinsic air sensitivity and sluggish kinetics originating from the spontaneous lattice Na extn. during air exposure and high tetrahedral site energy of Na+ diffusion transition state.  What is worse, the improvement on the two handicaps is hard to simultaneously realize because of the contradiction between Na containment suggested in air stability mechanism and enhanced Na diffusion mentioned in kinetics strategy.  Herein, it is shown that a simple strategy of introducing proper Na vacancies into lattice can simultaneously realize a dual performance improvement.  Na vacancies decrease the charge d. on transitional metal ions and enhance the antioxidative capability of material, ensuring a stable lattice Na containment for Na0.93Li0.12Ni0.25Fe0.15Mn0.48O2 when exposed to air.  Addnl., more Na+ diffusional sites and enlarged Na layer spacing are obtained and result in a significantly decreased energy barrier from ≈1000 to 300 meV and a high rate capability of 70.8% retention at 2000 mA g-1.  Remarkably, such a strategy can be easily realized by either pre- or post-treating, which exhibits excellent universality for various O3 materials, implying its enormous potential to promote the com. application of O3-type cathodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.1c00804"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ruan, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enabling a Stable Room-Temperature Sodium-Sulfur Battery Cathode by Building Heterostructures in Multichannel Carbon Fibers</span>. <i>ACS Nano</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>15</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5639</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5648</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enabling a Stable Room-Temperature Sodium-Sulfur Battery Cathode by Building Heterostructures in Multichannel Carbon Fibers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ye, Xin; Ruan, Jiafeng; Pang, Yuepeng; Yang, Junhe; Liu, Yongfeng; Huang, Yizhong; Zheng, Shiyou</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. sodium-sulfur (RT Na-S) batteries are widely considered as one of the alternative energy-storage systems with low cost and high energy d.  However, the both poor cycle stability and capacity are two crit. issues arising from low conversion kinetics and sodium polysulfides (NaPSs) dissoln. for sulfur cathodes during the charge/discharge process.  Herein, we report a highly stable RT Na-S battery cathode via building heterostructures in multichannel carbon fibers.  The TiN-TiO2@MCCFs, fabricated by electrospinning and nitriding techniques, are loaded with the active material S, forming S/TiN-TiO2@MCCFs as the cathode in a RT Na-S battery.  At 0.1 A g-1, the cathode produces the capacity of more than 640 mAh g-1 within 100 cycles with a high Coulombic efficiency of nearly 100%.  Even at 5 A g-1, the battery still exhibites a capacity of 257.1 mAh g-1 after 1000 cycles.  Combining structural and electrochem. analyses with the first-principles calcns. reveals that the incorporation of the highly electrocatalytic activity of TiN with the powerful chemisorption of TiO2 well stabilizes S and also alleviates the shuttle effects of polysulfides.  This work with simple processes and low cost is expected to promote the further development and application of metal-S batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.9b03467"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tunable Redox Chemistry and Stability of Radical Intermediates in 2D Covalent Organic Frameworks for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tunable Redox Chemistry and Stability of Radical Intermediates in 2D Covalent Organic Frameworks for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gu, Shuai; Wu, Shaofei; Cao, Lujie; Li, Minchan; Qin, Ning; Zhu, Jian; Wang, Zhiqiang; Li, Yingzhi; Li, Zhiqiang; Chen, Jingjing; Lu, Zhouguang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Radicals are inevitable intermediates during the charging and discharging of org. redox electrodes.  The increase of the reactivity of the radical intermediates is desirable to maximize the capacity and enhance the rate capability but is detrimental to cycling stability.  Therefore, it is a great challenge to controllably balance the redox reactivity and stability of radical intermediates to optimize the electrochem. properties with a good combination of high specific capacity, excellent rate capability, and long-term cycle life.  Herein, we reported the redox and tunable stability of radical intermediates in covalent org. frameworks (COFs) considered as high capacity and stable anode for sodium-ion batteries.  The comprehensive characterizations combined with theor. simulation confirmed that the redox of C-O· and α-C radical intermediates play an important role in the sodiation/desodiation process.  Specifically, the stacking behavior could be feasibly tuned by the thickness of 2D COFs, essentially detg. the redox reactivity and stability of the α-C radical intermediates and their contributive capacity.  The modulation of reversible redox chem. and stabilization mechanism of radical intermediates in COFs offers a novel entry to design novel high performance org. electrode materials for energy storage and conversion.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2022.01.023"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ko, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cho, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">To facilitate the practical realization of sodium-ion batteries, the energy d., detd. by the output operating voltage and/or capacity, needs to be improved to the level of com. Li-ion batteries.  Herein, O3-type Na0.98Ca0.01[Ni0.5Mn0.5]O2 is synthesized by incorporating Ca2+ into the NaO6 octahedron of Na[Ni0.5Mn0.5]O2 and its potential use as a cathode material for high energy d. SIBs is demonstrated.  The ionic radius of calcium (≈1.00 Å) is similar to that of sodium (≈1.02 Å); hence, it is energetically favorable for calcium to occupy sites in the sodium layers.  Within a wide operating voltage range of 2.0-4.3 V, O3-type Na0.98Ca0.01[Ni0.5Mn0.5]O2 exhibits a reversible O3-P3-O3 phase transition with small vol. changes compared to Ca-free Na[Ni0.5Mn0.5]O2 because of the strong interaction between Ca2+ and O2- and delivers a high reversible capacity of 209 mA h g-1 at 15 mA g-1 with improved cycling stability.  Moreover, Ca substitution improves the practically useful aspects such as thermal and air stability.  A prototype pouch full cell with a hard carbon anode shows an excellent capacity retention of 67% over 300 cycles.  Thus, this study provides an efficient and simple method to boost the performance and applicability of layered oxide cathode materials for practical applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/tcr.201800125"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Watanabe, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, S.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Crystallographic Evolution of P2 Na2/3Fe0.4Mn0.6O2 Electrodes during Electrochemical Cycling</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dose, Wesley M.; Sharma, Neeraj; Pramudita, James C.; Kimpton, Justin A.; Gonzalo, Elena; Han, Man Huon; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
